Spinster was still put on my daughter's UK marriage certificate as her status as she entered to sign it. That was in the year 2000.I  As a child in the 50s, I heard the word a lot but it wasn't an insult. Women would say, oh, I'm a spinster, to which married women with all the ties of a large family and a home without much in the way of labour saving equipment, would often reply with slight envy. Many unmarried women had nicer clothing, a home that was more feminine and tidier than married women tended to have and the freedom to buy, wear and eat as they chose, so they we're envied. How times change. But do I feel constrained by being 70 myself now? No! I don't wear old lady clothes, I wear classic clothing. I don't feel old, don't behave as an old woman, so I am not treated as one by younger people. Much of how well you are viewed at any age has to do with attitude.

Coincidentally, I found this video just now, at 39, I'm set to turn 40 next month (ignore the pic, it's extremely old, I was 27 in this pic lol), and I feel like I'm kinda at a weird reinvention crossroads when it comes to my style. I feel like it's taken on a more bold edge than it has in a while. In addition to this, my body has more constraints than it used to due to my disabilities and chronic illnesses. I get more stomach bloating from my condition due to the amount of salt that I have to consume to keep my vitals right.  I get dizzy from my condition so I can't wear heels like I used to. I love shoes and sometimes it's more work finding a sexy flat that works with my smaller narrower feet and still has support (and boo on all those ugly shapeless ballet flats that curl up and have no support in their weak sole, 2005 called and wants them back, they should have STAYED gone). Also, my joints are more floppy and end up putting me in way more pain so there are times I've had to put kt tape on myself, which can be visible. My vascular system is a mess and I have to keep the blood returning up with pretty strong compression stockings.   But I'm adamant that I'm going to do whatever to keep myself from looking so-called "matronly" because I'm not ready to excessively age that much as of just yet. Because part of the understanding of disability too is that it doesn't just happen to elderly people, and a lot of things are marketed to elderly people without any kind of regards to fashion or taste at all, like they assume that you get old and that you just... give up. Or disappear. which is what they also want disabled people to do as well. Which is the antithesis to what I believe. I will do anything but disappear. I was made to stand out, and I will.   But when it comes to disability being visible or invisible, with the exceptions of some places, stuff isn't designed with being a fashionable disabled person in mind (there are, of course, exceptions, like the cane brand SwitchSticks, which comes in fashionable designs and I have a cane stool with a fancy design on it that ALL the old ladies ask me about). If you look at some stuff, like strong enough compression stockings though (by strong enough, I mean at least 20-30, if not 30-40 strength, and aside from some poorly marked runner stockings on amazon that may not even be accurately above 20 strength, and most anything fashionable that claims to be "compression" is only usually 8-15 which isn't anywhere close to strong enough), at the very firm strength, you get mostly two options:.... "plain black"... and "medicalized beige" (which usually matches nobody's skin tone aside from the oranging undertones of maybelline's dream matte mousse foundation, and I'm sure some older millennials are chuckling because they KNOW exactly what it is that I'm talking about since that foundation turned everyone orange). I've done things to adapt to the clashing of my less matching compression stockings, I've covered them up with sexy flat knee high boots, I've put knit fishnet style stockings over them that disguised them, other types of socks or stockings over them, or opted for the strongest sheer ones I could find (allegro, evonation, and jomi are some brands that I've bought my more sheer or fashionable fancy compression stockings from, and although almost passable, all the beige ones are still darker than my actual skintone).   I'm having to be choicier about my fashion than I used to be due to these disability constraints, but, at the same time, for whatever reason, I feel more fashionable now than I have ever been my whole life as well. When I was young, I used to buy whatever was in style at goordmans or deb. or rainbow. or sears, or penneys. Whatever. But now I know what my style is at this point, what works for me, and what aesthetic I follow. I also know what will last the test of time (aka not stuff with loose sequins or PU stuff that falls apart in a few years time). Granted, I'm also here to ride a little bit of the revival of the Y2K fashion aesthetic wave the second time around in some regards, because some of those fashions are fun when it comes to textural variation as well as shapes. I also love the amount of color in recent fashion as well. I also I aim to make things effortless in a confident way.  Maybe one of these days I can be one of those fashionable old ladies at the art museum. That's my aim. Because the day I stop being fashionable is the day I die.

Regarding the rituals of passage for girls as they age, I'm latina and quincea√±eros were historically used to present girls in society as they got to a certain age. There's even the tradition of changing shoes from a casual no heel shoe to high heels in the party, to represent the girl being finally of age to wear them. These days it's optional and I didn‚Äôt do it, but many of my classmates did. As for marriage & hair, in ancient china once a woman got married she'd only use her hair up in buns, while younger girls were expected to always have it down. It's interesting to see how different cultures interpret aging and how it hasn't always had a negative connotation!

Maybe it's because I'm older but I like Clinton Kelly's take. It's not that more mature women can't participate in trends it's more that we're less likely to be drawn into a style simply because it's trendy. We know what we like and what suits us. It also becomes easier to look at fashion and see what's genuinely good design and what's silly and going to be stylish for five minutes and disappear forever. Over time it becomes more obvious that some things will always look good, some things may go out but will always be back before long and what people will be rolling their eyes about in two years.
When you‚Äôre younger most women want to attract a mate, hence wanting to look young (fashionable) and as good and as possible. When you are older that‚Äôs less of an issue for most, so as long as you feel comfortable and look how you want to, you care much less about fashion and what others think about you. Obviously there are some women who are really drawn to fashion so want to be fashionable, which is cool, but most just want to feel good in themselves. You are more concerned about health as you get older not fashion.

I started making my own clothing in grammar school because I was a very small size but had slightly longer legs and arms.  Manufactures believed back then that if you were a tiny size you must also be short, I was not.  Clothing back then was always too short on me and the only way I got some that did not look like I out grew them was to make them with longer sleeves and hems.  I fell out of making clothing in my 30's because the style changed to sleeve cuffs being half way down the back of your hand and I finally could get them long enough and woman's jeans started coming in leg lengths.  Now I am in my 70"s and returning to making them again to get better colors and higher quality fabrics ( not throw away fashions) that fit and give me joy and self confidence in the way I want too look. As an older woman I will tell you one reason we shouldn't blithely wear maxi skirts is because we will trip in them if they get between our legs (which they will!) and such. If our shoe heel catches on them when we bend over etc.  If we wore petticoats which I'm glad we don't, then we could keep a skirt that's longer from flapping between our legs and catching us unawares. We wouldn't be able to keep our heal from catching in it or tripping on it with the toe of our shoe from bending over, assuming we can bend over! üòä  A great deal of fashion changes we make as we get older is because our clothes don't serve us the way we need them, as we are less coordinated at times, weaker, long sleeves get in the way if they drape anywhere near the wrist, and we need to be able to move freely because we may be in pain with our backs etc. We also need for our clothing to not catch on things as we may be more apt to catch on railings or edges of car doors and if you get pulled back as a weaker older person, it can cause you to stagger trip and fall.   It's not that hard for fashion designers to make things that would work for us!  What galls me is that with what we now know about cut and styling, with the wonderful diverse fabrics we have, something like even a mumu dress could be made to have a bit more flattering shape. As well as diverse options for shaping for different body styles for older women. We are NOT all the same!  It's not that difficult - designers could make a fortune doing things like this. And so could alterations people if they would not charge too much, we could bring all of our clothes to them to be slightly reshaped for flattery for our own body type. People used to have their clothes altered all the time if they didn't do it themselves.   That's the key to looking nice as we grow older Imo. Wear what you want, make sure it's serving your body and movements well, and pay attention to the silhouette, how it hangs on your changed body and go with the flow!

There are deeper reasons to why women tend to wear more relaxed clothes as they age. One of them has to do with mobility. As we age, in general, our bodies tend to have a more restricted range of movement. Clothes that have more movement and space allow older people to move in unrestricted ways. Same happens with shoes. That's why as we age, we tend to look for functionality vs style trends.  It doesn't mean that women have to look, as they say, FRUMPY, but is a reality of life! As we age, our bodies change and clothes need to cater that those needs. I'm 52 years young and I have never liked restricted clothing. Also, I live in a hot tropical climate. At this point in my life, I still wear my own very personal style, but many times I feel to wear something light, flowy, and with elastic waitsband üòâ. And I'm fit and on the slimmer side.

A spinster was a skilled woman who spun fibre into thread.  She married late because she was too valuable to lose from the family‚Äôs hand weaving business.  When she did marry it would be to an older man (her own age) who had his own house which incorporated a loom.  Because she married relatively late, she usually survived pregnancy and childbirth well and many lived into old age. Spinster appears in the ‚Äòoccupation‚Äô column of parish registers so frequently as it was one of the few women‚Äôs jobs recognised.  It was therefore an indication of value rather than anything else. It was only when spinning and weaving became industrialised that ‚Äòspinster‚Äô took on its modern meaning.

Great and insightful commentary as always, but as a practicing Hindu woman, I'd like to clarify that the Bindi is not a symbol of marital status, but a marker of faith, much like wearing a cross might be, and has no real bearing on age or maturity. Young girls can and do wear a bindi, as do women of all ages. Hindu men also mark their forehead for much the same reason as a bindi is worn by women- often traditionally kumkum north India (or those in the diaspora hailing from the north), or vibhuthi or 'namam' in the south of India (or diaspora).  While today, bindi-wearing is practiced less, particularly in north India, or those in the diaspora abroad, and has sometimes been adopted by wearers of other faiths as a 'fashion statement', this to me is the same as wearing a hijab as a fashion statement - it's just not, at least for the majority? While progressiveness is always positive, calling a religious symbol a fashion statement feels flippant at best, and reads to me as dilution and appropriation. Perhaps it's purist of me, but a bindi is still first and foremost symbol of the Hindu faith, in a way that's widely practiced still today, with no link to age or marital status.   There are of course plenty of other visual markers of a woman's age or marital status in Hindu and Indian culture. For example Sindoor, a red forehead marking often worn by women in north India,  is often a symbol of marriage, and perhaps this is what you meant rather than the bindi. There's also certain drapes of a saree that are traditionally reserved for married women, and several other symbols of maturity or marriage, but a bindi really isn't one of them.   Sorry for the essay, but I regularly keep up with your videos and was quite disappointed to see this, so really needed to set the record straight so my culture isn't misrepresented! :D
Gosh, who even talks about this? Thankyou!‚ù§

There are so many ways this topic can be approached.  For instance, clothing depends a lot on climate, unless you are constantly indoors in a modern city.  Consider how Pacific Islanders dress, at any age. Or, consider cultures that wear very few, if any clothes, like some Australian first nations people (there are lots of different ones), who often wear only a sort of string around the waist, plus or minus various body paints or ornaments.  Then there is what people do with their hair - e.g. some tribes in New Guinea. In Brisbane, Australia, where I am now, old people wear shorts because it is hot. You just cannot wear a ton of clothing, although I notice that young people here actually wear boots as a fashion accessory sometimes, perhaps because they can control their body temperature really well. I cannot even bear closed shoes here in spring and summer. In Melbourne, Australia, on very hot days, people tend to drop otherwise somewhat conservative and self-conscious forms of dress, and go tropical.  Much of the time, though, they wear mass-produced leisurewear, i.e. untailored one-size shirts and sweat pants.  There has been very little local clothing design and production since the 1970s here, and the style, variety, and quality have greatly declined.  I noticed this in France too.  When I lived there in the 1970s, there were all sorts of lovely clothes, but in the 2000s, it's all one-size imported, along with McDonalds and rising obesity.
I'm a 48 year old woman, and I've always looked younger than my age, so I hear, "wow, you look so young for your age!", as though that's a compliment, as though looking my actual age is somehow a bad thing. I'm rediscovering my style from my 20s, because I love it and dressing in a more "alternative" way makes me happy. I'm going to wear whatever I want, criticism be damned. I'm a lot more confident now than I was then, so I will be even more brave with my wardrobe than I was back then. If I want to wear a short skirt with thigh-high stockings, then I won't let anyone, especially my own inner critic, tell me no. I've wasted too much time denying myself the styles I love in order to look "proper and appropriate for my age", and the older I get, the more  I realize that life is too short and precious to weigh myself down with the weight of others' expectations and preferences. Dress for yourself, whatever that means for you. At the end of the day, all you have is yourself living and aging in your body. You can play with clothing, makeup, and accessories however you wish. Never let anyone take that pleasure away from you.

I'm 53 and I don't feel this pressure from society at all. It may have been true in the past but now it's all "Yes girl, slay Kween". A lot of your quotes and visuals are from generations ago.  There is pressure on me in the current time to dress as if I were younger and I do not want to. It is true that I am dealing with changing proportions. I do not care about trends, I care about what is flattering.  I also want to dress more modestly. Your point that women have less value reproductively as they age is just a biological fact. From my perspective as an older woman, it is not oppressive. For me, it comes with a sense of freedom and empowerment. It frees up so much time. Younger women want to emphasize their reproductive assets. I have no interest in dressing that way. I am not interested in dressing for the opposite sex, nor do I want to be involved in the catty competition with other women. My hormones are no longer raging and I am more interested in my community service and building up society.  Your priorities shift with age. I dress professionally, my appearance reflecting the wisdom and competence that I have acquired. You are young, you are lovely and I covet your blouse. But of course, you are not our age yet and you don't understand our perspective.

Just as an aside: Post menopausal women do not lose their value evolutionarily. It's extremely rare to have a species that survives post-menopause at all, but because humans are social animals that thrive because of our long childhoods spent learning about the world, the value of a community member who is no longer caring for their own offspring is huge.

Here's a thing. I dyed my hair constantly (various colors) since I was 15. I stopped at some point in my 30s due to not feeling like putting in the effort. Now that I'm 48, I don't dye my hair because I dislike the idea of giving the impression that I'm trying to hide something. The hair color thing is part of my general personal preference for not trying to pass off fake things as real. I'm fine with wearing fake things. I have a fake pearl necklace that looks like it and I love it. I would rather wear something fuzzy and obviously synthetic than wear fake fur that tries to look like actual fur, if that makes sense. I feel that can be the difference between making a personal style statement and trying to appear to be something you're not.  Similarly, it's worth keeping in mind that wearing "granny" clothes when you're young is a statement. Wearing the same clothes when you're an actual granny may be intended as a statement, but is less likely to communicate your intent clearly, and I prefer my choices to be clearly my choices.  It's also worthwhile considering that cyclical fashion trends mean that some stuff that looks fresh and new to young people may look drab and boring to those who went through that particular cycles the previous time(s) is came around. We can get bored with stuff we saw everyone wear already, and not necessarily feel like repeating it. The same goes for other aspects of design. The Mid Century Modern furniture trend looks to many older people like the stuff all the old boring relatives (from a child's perspective) had in their homes when they were kids, and they don't necessarily want to recreate that in their homes. For me it would be like going back to my student apartment with my parents hand-me-down furniture set. I am just not interested.   Bottom line, the things academia or the fashion industry or the younger side of the internet think of as the problems facing older women are not necessarily what actually bother us. Now, universally non-creasing concealer, on the other hand...
I don't think that "do what you want but expect some reactions' is a contradictory statement. I think it's trivial.   I'm perimenopausal, and though my body is changing in weird and unexpected ways, which is definitely a learning curve, I think it's silly to follow rules that tell you what you should or shouldn't wear (though getting tips from women who've been through it can be useful). I think it's equally silly to choose to wear attention-grabbing or unconventional clothes and then be all shocked Pikachu face when you get attention.  I wore a lot of unique clothes and dyed my hair a variety of unnatural colors when I was younger because I loved the way they made me feel. I got more looks from strangers for looking the way I wanted to than I probably would've gotten had I worn a pair of jeans and a T shirt. It is what it is. People look at things that are less common or more eye catching, and they may or may not approve. So, if that's what you love, this shouldn't stop you, but it should be expected. Trivial, non contradictory, not bad advice, really.

Following fashion of now seems to repeat earlier repeats of earlier fashion. The prehistory humans were just following the herds in a slow loop that shifted a little bit in one direction or another every revolution and 10,000 years later there's indigenous Americans or doggerlanders. "Spinster" was just the equivalent of "bachelor" - denoting someone who had never been married. "Spinster" was on marriage documents well into the 1900s (I'm not sure what they use in modern times) to record whether a woman had been previously married and was now a widow (or a divorcee in more recent times). It had absolutely nothing to do with age. You were a spinster till you married, just as men were bachelors until they married. Its negative connotations began when it was used derogatively to describe older women who'd never been married, because to society, an unmarried woman was useless, and how dare a woman be 55 and unmarried (whether she would have liked to be or not). Fashion's despising of older women is really just an aspect of society's general hatred for women. Women are tolerable to men when they're young, hot playthings or producing babies for a man. Otherwise society would prefer they drop dead before the first wrinkle or health problem begins.

I once read a piece about how attitudes toward women's age and bodies with regards to fashion really significantly changed after the 60s and the cocaine chic, Twiggy look became popular. Before that, there was value in looking "mature" - it wasn't matronly, and it certainly wasn't frumpy. Mature styles were structured and elegant. But after the 60s, there was a huge emphasis on youth, and marketing turned towards teenage fashion. This left older women in the dust as eventually fashion designers lost the knowledge and resources on dressing "older" women in a fashionable way. What you thematise at the beginning of your video as "that's how it was" is based more on fictionional storytelling than fact. Moreover, you emphasise the exception to the rule rather than the rule that women have always considered themselves equal to men, never their property and not the victims they were portrayed as. They used their means at their disposal.  To tell a contemporary industrial labourer that she was her husband's property would have earned you the response that you were out of your depth. Because in fact she lived in just as poor conditions as her husband and children. To have said this to a farmer's wife from the pre-industrialisation era would have given you the answer that she ran a farm together with her husband - she knew he couldn't do without her in the same way she couldn't do without him.   To have said this to a woman from a wealthy family whose husband was living off the earnings of the former farmers and the later industrial workers, she would have - and indeed she had - let the flea be put in her ear of completely forgetting that she enjoyed every conceivable protection and chivalry, never had to go to war, never had to cook, raise her kids without staff, you name it.   You use this image of the oppressed woman as virtue signalling. You invite all the comments who "poor me" themselves and worse, give young women the image that they are under priviledged or otherwise being taken advantage of - like from the fashion industry. The industry does not "hate", it sells what sells best.   I am a woman of 54. If I want to have compliments, I need to dress up and to make up for it. Otherwise, I will be overlooked. Now, that is how life goes. It need not bother me in one way or the other. If I am fine with my age and weight, nothing can insult me, especially no insults being made by other females. Which, indeed, is much more often the case than being insulted by men.   Men have loved women throughout all ages, they have written great literature and poetry about them, they have adored them as women as well appreciated them as mothers. Movies take the stylistic device of drama (because, after all, that is how stories sell) - better not to take them as a common reality (but a particular one, also vastly differing timewise, localwise and culture wise).   To deduce a general - and not a particular - structure of disadvantages for women from these unscientific observations is as dishonest as it is factually incorrect. It would be correct to say that women were at a disadvantage in certain social and legal matters, just as men were at a disadvantage to them in terms of their social responsibilities, whether it was ostracism due to offences in business and political life or the arguably more serious disadvantage of going to war as soldiers. As well as very hard and physically demanding labour that no average woman would ever be able to do.

At time stamp 19:37 through 20:00‚Ä¶.  Do I say it or let it be?‚Ä¶ ugh, I can‚Äôt help myself!  I‚Äôll limit my comment to the Downton Abbey dig toward management of Violet Crawley‚Äôs wardrobe.  Ok, 3 things.  First, when it comes to historical fashion, especially around turn of the century and into the early 1900s, fashion admittedly was becoming ‚Äúfaster,‚Äù however, clothes were still made to last and were somewhat size-adjustable, and tailors (or an individual‚Äôs sewing ability) were a common resource to update existing clothing in one‚Äôs wardrobe to coordinate with newer trends and changes in body size in lieu of tossing clothes and buying new ones (Abby Cox and other dress historians on YouTube have videos on this).  Second, the grandmother in Downton Abbey is proud of her heritage, status, and tradition, so of all personalities, she would be the type to not change her dress to match the latest trends.  AAAAND, if you think about it, people often like the music/style/trends that were popular in their most formative years and, if they are intentional about it, find ways to incorporate pieces of it into their style years after it‚Äôs ‚Äúcool.‚Äù (Ex. my dad still listens to 70s rock music, the queen of England‚ÄîGod rest her soul‚Äîwore fashionable 40s style matching skirt suits and hats for decades after they were in fashion.  I hope you catch my drift.)  All I‚Äôm saying is, while the statement that Hollywood is dressing older women a certain way may be true, Downton Abbey was not the right reference to prove that since A) the traditional outfit fit the character‚Äôs preference, B) clothes in that time period were made to survive decades of wear and tear and body changes, and given the 1910s setting, Violet‚Äôs late 1800s wardrobe would be historically accurate considering she grew up in a different decade from the rest and takes pride in that, and C) Violet was FAR from invisible.  She was quite assertive and made her presence known with her cutting remarks and wise advice being the most memorable part of many episodes.  Not just here to troll. This video is brilliantly researched! (and scary cause women, afabs, and femmes have it hard enough) But just a nitpick, it's pronounced more like moh koh than moe koe. It's really nice to see non New Zealanders talk about Maori Culture! Just wanted to let people know how to pronounce it accurately
Huh- admittedly I am a white New Zealander, but I was always told that moko were tattoos that MƒÅori women got specifically when they were considered an ‚Äúelder‚Äù (in the sense that they were respected as someone who was wise and/or as a leader). The tattoo being done specifically around the mouth (moko kauae) is a symbol of the power of their speech/opinion and a sign that they should be listened to, as well as granting them authority to speak during gatherings held at the marae (meeting grounds, like a town hall but with spiritual connotations not unlike a church. Not just anybody can speak out or give speeches there). In general moko signifies social status wherever it is on the body, but I guess it‚Äôs important to note that the rules differ between regions and tribes. Also, MƒÅori have kinda had to reinvent a lot of it since their old customs were disrupted by NZ‚Äôs colonisation. Especially when it comes to specific designs, some were and are so locale specific that you can tell exactly which place/tribe the person is from.  If any MƒÅori happen upon my comment please correct me if I got something wrong! But just in case, I wanted to flag that moko kauae is much more nuanced than just separating girls from women üòä

Love your videos (especially the historical ones) and I have a video request. WOMEN HORSE-RIDERS FASHION: THE MASCULINE, THE FEMININE AND THE REALLY UNSAFE. I am currently writing a thesis on Victorian masculinity and horse-riding (in literature, not fashion), and I came across an article titled ‚ÄúElegant Amazons: Victorian Riding Habits and the Fashionable Horsewoman.‚Äù And they said something that should have been obvious to me but I had never considered it. Women at the time rode side-saddle because their skirts (and good ol fashioned propriety) did not allow them to sit astride like a normal person. Apparently this was dangerous, not only because this position is not so stable (see what I did there?) but because the skirt could get caught in the saddle or the horse's legs, and the woman could get dragged behind a running steed. Consequently, Victorian riding garments for women became of of the most complex forms of attire to sew and wear. There is also a disscussion of the subversiveness of these clothes, which meshed masculine elements into the dress. Super interesting Now I know that bikes gave rise to bloomers' fashion and generally opened the way for respectable women to wear pants, but this article made me really intrigued about the wole issue and I would love a video, because this is super interesting but really outside the scope of my own research.  Here is a citation for the article I mentioned: David, Alison Matthews. ‚ÄúElegant Amazons: Victorian Riding Habits and the Fashionable Horsewoman.‚Äù Victorian Literature and Culture, vol. 30, no. 1, 2002, pp. 179‚Äì210.

Clothes manufacturers and the fashion industry in general don't like older women because we grew up wearing classy, well-made garments that we often made ourselves. We knew about fabrics, and draping, and seam allowances. We could take up or let down a hem. Most of these skills are all but lost now.  Modern clothes are poorly made, mass produced, expensive, synthetic rubbish. Few people look good in them without the aid of a talented photographer and a strong dose of Photoshop. So older women (and anyone else who doesn't fit the mold) are made to feel inadequate, because we might call their bluff.  I'm 70, I've always had my own style. My educated guess is demographic spending. I'd assume younger folks spend more on clothing and that purchase rate dwindles as they get older. On top of that, people die, so if you have 1m 18 under buying $50-500 in clothes a month, you might have 200k or less 40+ making a purchase for themselves every few months. Then when you look at the people who are still fashion forward at that age, it's even less. So like, why design, why market, why spend significant time and money targeting that demo. Sure there's some money there, but in the upper eschelon of spending you have companies that serve all ages like Sperry Topsider and then smaller botiques that sell small run funky designers. So you're usually looking at a smaller and more stressed ecosystem of buyers and sellers. I wouldn't necessarily say there's less money going around (though there probably is,) but there's definitely a much smaller volume of purchases. Again, I'm just guessing. But 99% of the time when people are looking at consumer facing companies and are like "Why didn't they _____" it usually starts with demographic spending behavior, because that's how the marketing and sales team break down analytic data to inform future business decisions. This choice to "abandon" certain demos then reinforces the initial decision, because some people if they don't have anything good available, give up or find other avenues to do it.  On top of that, there's the manufacturing side (i.e. cost cutting.) It's really efficient (time and money) to get really good at making only 1 thing over and over again, just drive down cost as much as possible. It's really expensive to make 1 different thing for everyone. Sometimes to a point where it's not viable to maintain the corporate overhead that goes into marketing, designing, researching, just to get a one off of something into someone's hands. For something like a macbook, you might have millions in R&D. If you sold just one macbook, you'd need to sell it for like $10m just to break even. That's sort of how Shein "revolutionized" the clothing pipeline. They basically just rip off other designers, cutting R&D prices immensely, and then use very similar materials and stitching across all of their products (plastic). So when they're making a huge amount of products, it's still labor intensive etc, but they don't have to spend nearly as much as any other company does trying to come up with original designs.  I love this channel so much, but it's important to recognize that the Board of Directors at H&M could literally give a shit if Ethel in Minnesota has access to grunge chic granny core. They are just looking at giant balance sheets and talking to department heads about ROI/budget and operating concerns, as well as future plans for the company and growth sectors.  Most of the things that consumers care about only become a line item for a larger company when there's a demonstrated either drop in sales due to ignoring it, or potential new area of profit due to focusing on it.
As a queer person I'm unlearning this message that in order to be attractive you have to look younger, and learning to love getting older. Since I'm queer I view getting older as a blessing because so many queer people haven't gotten the chance to grow older and become elders. Too many have died from AIDS, hate crimes, suicide, etc, so that the more of us who reach our 50s, 60s, 70s+ become a story of survival and queer joy and resistance in a society that increasingly wants us to disappear. To all my queer folks reading this embrace aging, embrace the greying and wrinkles. We are here and we should celebrate that

PSA: The goal of advertising media isnt to glorify youth, specifically. It is to market unattainable standards to make us all feel insecure and buy 'solutions' (aka beauty products). Even at the peak of youth, women get targeted ads that say they are not enough. The anti-ageing media is simply a continuation of that. There is no magic age at which marketing caters to you and makes you feel great about yourself. Because thats not the point.  The fashion industry, especially mainstream modelling, often seeks to exclude anyone that isn't skinny, young,  conventionally attractive, able-bodied, cis, and has Eurocentric features. Then, when they get models that tick those boxes, they edit them into oblivion. On that basis, they create an unattainable ideal that excludes basically everyone.

Ageism is very real, but I can tell you this, every single young woman I have ever talked to about this, has a fierce fascination and appreciation for older women, especially on the level of style and fashion. Every. Single. One. If you approach the topic without disdain, or reproach, women and girls are fascinated with the je ne sais quoi of older women (or, their sunglasses, their brooches, their...). Social capital, and conditioning backed by fear, has turned many a person into a bit of a villain, trying to protect what they have, but on the flip side, people try to imagine what it means to be old, and what it could mean for them to get old. That is powerful.  I'll tell you something: Becoming an individual is like taking a space walk, no matter how you feel on the journey, whether you're frozen in panic or not, whether you want to go back, or not, whether you drift off to your death, or not, whether you make it back safely, or not. Everyone looking on is impressed, in the sense that they feel an impression being made by what you are doing, on a part of who they are going forwards. Some are terrified. Some are minimizing, apathetic, and cynical. Some want and need you to succeed so they know they can too. Some feel awe. They see the raw scope you are navigating, the whole of space a suit's width away from you. Some are in love with your bravery. Some are in love with the bravery of the notion of individuating at all. Some are helpful, focused on the next best action to take, and communicating in the right way. Some will keep a secret piece of you, and your experience tucked up in their heart. Some will cheer you on, and watch breathlessly. Some will ball up their fists, and watch you carefully, like they would help you get it right, if only they had a com line to you, and your nervous system, but they don't. Ultimately, it's going to be you, in your suit, facing space, facing this experience, counting on yourself, hoping that people partnering you will be a reliable help, especially when it comes to activities that take multiple people to execute. But, somehow, out of all the different outcomes, and all the scattered reactions, the glory of your space walk is crafted... But, somehow, deep down, you always knew it was there. You always knew it was glorious. Somehow you know everyone who faces that same space walk knows it too, deep down, and they keep that knowledge with just an extra little bit of an impression of you, on your mission.   So people are scared. They reject you. They throw entitled temper tantrums, and they don't have the decency to realize, or apologize. What they do is in no way acceptable. However, your power is not in what they do, or don't do: It is in how you leave an impression, joining with that knowledge they have underneath it all. No amount of thrashing about is going to change the nature of that, or diminish it. Neither will that seem particularly useful to you as you seek decency, and dignity, and safety, but you too, have that knowledge of glory deep down, and when you align with it, you have everything any individual life, any space walk can give you, or the world. You have all the power, all the meaning, awake within you, as you. How could a person not be fascinated with that? How could a person not be in love with that?

Errata: world "spinster" and the job of spinster dates even earlier, back to medieval times. But - and that's the clue actually - why single lady is "spinster", not "laundress" or hatmaker or whatever? Cause before inventing spinning wheel, spindle whorls were in use. This tool requires much, much, MUCH more labour & time to create a thread. And that means: there were a lot of job positions to do that. A lot. One weaver (more skilled, profitable job position than spinster, so of course traditionally reserved for men...) required EIGHT spinsters to make thread for him, to be able to weave textiles (1st necessity product) constantly and efficiently. That much job positions.  And it was work traditionally reserved for women. So it was the easiest and the fastest way for a woman to earn her own money, but especially in times of spindle whorl, not as much in spinning wheel times, as on Mina's icons. "Spinster" started to have negative connotation and meaning of the world altered - but later on. Earlier - if you didn't want or could marry, for any reason - go to be a spinster. Live your life independently and happily, here's a good, paid job to achieve that - and that's it. There were zero negative connotations to it at the beginning (then it changed of course, bc patriarchy), it was just so often correlated with being single, it started to be used interchangeably. So things were similar as Mina says - but even earlier ;)
